doc_id=3352 igt_id=igt3352-59 3603 3619 L G B B B B M M M M M M M B M B T+AC
language: Latin (lat)
line=3603 tag=L:  (107) [Eam                 nunc            esse         inventam                   probris                conpotem]                 scis.
line=3604 tag=G:           ANA-F-S-ACC       now             be-INFIN     found-PPP -S-F-ACC       bad deed-N-PL-ABL        sharing in-F-S-ACC        know-2-S
line=3605 tag=B:
line=3606 tag=B:
line=3607 tag=B:
line=3608 tag=B:
line=3609 tag=M:           22
line=3610 tag=M:              Ted is an allomorph of te, frequently occurring before a vowel or /h/. However, unlike English an, it is
line=3611 tag=M:  not mandatory before a word beginning with a vowel, as shown by the sequence te esse in the first clause in (106).
line=3612 tag=M:  Here the two variants can probably be attributed to poetic meter. (With a /d/, te esse would not elide into /tesse/, and
line=3613 tag=M:  without the /d/ ted erga would elide; those forms would not fit properly into the verse.) Possibly, Pacuvius chooses
line=3614 tag=M:  the two allomorphs to formally represent the different grammatical roles played by te in the sentence. Notably, ted
line=3615 tag=M:  appears often in early Latin but disappears by the classical period.
line=3616 tag=B:
line=3617 tag=M:                                                                               59
line=3618 tag=B:
line=3619 tag=T+AC:          `You know she has been found participating in bad behavior.' (Naev. Trag. 10-11)

line=4370 tag=L:  (132) Non         ego          [illam           mi        dotem...]          duco            [...esse...]   quae
line=4371 tag=G:           not      I-S-NOM      that-F-S-ACC     I-S-DAT   dowry-F-S-ACC      lead-1-S        be-INFIN       REL-F-S-NOM

line=4379 tag=B:
line=4380 tag=M:           29
line=4381 tag=M:               The variation between a and ab(s) for this preposition is phonologically conditioned similarly to English
line=4382 tag=M:  a ~ an, with the addition that the allomorph abs can occur epenthetically before an alveolar (e.g. abs te `from you' in
line=4383 tag=M:  Pl. Mil. 974 and Ter. And. 489, abs tergo `from the back' in Acc. Trag. 610).
line=4384 tag=M:            30
line=4385 tag=M:               The ablative as found from early Latin on includes the literal sense of `separation' (< ablatum `carried
line=4386 tag=M:  away from') as well as syncretism from several cases that were earlier distinct in PIE including the locative and the
line=4387 tag=M:  instrumental (Draeger, 1878, p. 1:495). Note that in example (131) the preposition cum in the PP cum armis aureis
line=4388 tag=M:  cannot mean `with' as an instrument; rather, it means `together with, accompanied by'.
line=4389 tag=M:            31
line=4390 tag=M:               English flee can express a source with or without a preposition (e.g. They fled (from) the hurricane);
line=4391 tag=M:  without the preposition, such a thematic role occurs in the same position as a direct object since it typically follows
line=4392 tag=M:  the verb. But Latin expresses the source in the ablative or genitive, never in the accusative.
line=4393 tag=B:
line=4394 tag=M:                                                                74
line=4395 tag=B:
line=4396 tag=L:           sedatum             cupidinem,        deum                metum,         parentum          amorem         et
line=4397 tag=G:           calm-PPP-M-S-ACC    desire-M-S-ACC    god-M-PL-GEN        fear-M-S-ACC   parent-M-PL-GEN   love-M-S-ACC   and
line=4398 tag=B:
line=4399 tag=L:           cognatum            concordiam].
line=4400 tag=G:           relative-M-PL-GEN   harmony-F-S-ACC
line=4401 tag=B:
line=4402 tag=T:           `I don't believe my dowry is that which is said [to be] a dowry, but rather chastity,
line=4403 tag=T:           modesty, calm desire, fear of the gods, love of one's parents and harmony among one's
line=4404 tag=T+AC:           relatives.' (Pl. Amph. 839-842)

doc_id=3352 igt_id=igt3352-86 4430 4456 L L L L L L B B B M M M M M M M B M B T T T T T T T T+AC
language: Latin (lat)
line=4430 tag=L:  (133) Propter eorum qui dicunt usum declinati casus, uti is qui de altero diceret, distinguere
line=4431 tag=L:        posset, cum vocaret, cum daret, cum accusaret32, sic alia eiudem discrimina, quae nos et
line=4432 tag=L:        Graecos ad declinandum duxerunt. . . Nos vero sex [casus] habemus, Graeci quinque:
line=4433 tag=L:        qui vocetur, ut Hercules; quemadmodum vocetur, ut Hercule; quo vocetur, ut ad
line=4434 tag=L:        Herculem; a quo vocetur, ut ab Hercule; cui vocetur, ut Herculi; cuius vocetur, ut
line=4435 tag=L:        Herculis.
line=4436 tag=B:
line=4437 tag=B:
line=4438 tag=B:
line=4439 tag=M:           32
line=4440 tag=M:              In translating his Greek sources, Varro inadvertently renders Greek  = aitiatik `effective,
line=4441 tag=M:  causative' as accusativus instead of the semantically correct causativus. (The two words are related in the legal
line=4442 tag=M:  sense of a `cause'.) In the 6th century AD the Roman grammarian Priscianus corrected the terminology, but by then
line=4443 tag=M:  Varro's had long become fixed in the grammatical lexicon. The original meaning is significant, as it refers to
line=4444 tag=M:  structural rather than inherent case assignment. In mistranslating the word, Varro changes the description to include
line=4445 tag=M:  only inherent case meanings.
line=4446 tag=B:
line=4447 tag=M:                                                                75
line=4448 tag=B:
line=4449 tag=T:         `The cases were derived for the use of those who speak, so that whoever spoke of another
line=4450 tag=T:         could distinguish when he was calling, when he was giving, when he was accusing, and
line=4451 tag=T:         similarly other distinctions of the same kind, which led us as well as the Greeks to the
line=4452 tag=T:         notion of declension. . . Anyway, we have six cases, the Greeks five: whoever is called,
line=4453 tag=T:         as Hercules [nominative]; how he is called, as Hercule [vocative]; to where he is called,
line=4454 tag=T:         as to Herculem [accusative]; by whom he is called, as by Hercule [ablative]; to [or, for]
line=4455 tag=T:         whom he is called, as Herculi [dative]; of whom [or, of what] he is called, Herculis
line=4456 tag=T+AC:         [genitive].' (Varro 8.6.16)

doc_id=3352 igt_id=igt3352-87 4499 4509 L L L L B T T T T T M+AC
language: Latin (lat)
line=4499 tag=L:  (134) Appellandi partes sunt quattuor, e quis dicta a quibusdam provocabula quae sunt ut quis,
line=4500 tag=L:        quae; vocabula ut scutum, gladium; nomina ut Romulus, Remus; pronomina ut hic, haec.
line=4501 tag=L:        Duo media dicuntur nominatus; prima et extrema articuli. Primum genus est infinitum,
line=4502 tag=L:        secundum ut infinitum, tertium ut finitum, quartum finitum.
line=4503 tag=B:
line=4504 tag=T:         `The kinds of naming are four, of which the words that are like quis, quae [`which'] are
line=4505 tag=T:         called provocables by some; those like scutum `shield' and gladium `sword' are called
line=4506 tag=T:         vocables; those like Romulus and Remus, names; those like hic, haec `this', pronouns.
line=4507 tag=T:         The two middle ones are called nouns; the first and last are articles. The first type is
line=4508 tag=T:         indefinite, the second is as if indefinite, the third is as if definite, the fourth definite.'
line=4509 tag=M+AC:         (Varro 8.45)

line=5759 tag=L:  (194) Tum               meo            patri                   autem          {torulus}   i
line=5760 tag=G:            then          my-M-S-DAT     father-M-S-DAT          however        knot-M-S-NOM    be in-3-S-FUT      golden-M-S-NOM        under
line=5761 tag=B:
line=5762 tag=B:
line=5763 tag=B:
line=5764 tag=B:
line=5765 tag=M:            36
line=5766 tag=M:               The literal meaning of this verb refers to the custom of a Roman paterfamilias `taking up' a newborn as
line=5767 tag=M:  his own. (Supposedly the child was placed on the ground in front of the doorway to the house, and it was the
line=5768 tag=M:  paterfamilias's prerogative to accept the child as his own or not.) In this way the child was literally `raised' by the
line=5769 tag=M:  father.
line=5770 tag=M:            37
line=5771 tag=M:               This is a pun on pecus `cattle' and grex `herd, flock'. Egregius is something literally `out of the flock',
line=5772 tag=M:  i.e. extraordinary in a good sense, based on the (patrician) Roman concept that the `crowd' is inferior to the
line=5773 tag=M:  (aristocratic) individual.
line=5774 tag=M:            38
line=5775 tag=M:               Although ea signa is plural, it clearly refers back to torulus aureus, as does the singular id signum. The
line=5776 tag=M:  failure of agreement in ea signa may be interpreted on the one hand as colloquial exaggeration and on the other as
line=5777 tag=M:  stylistic variation.
line=5778 tag=B:
line=5779 tag=M:                                                                                99

line=8602 tag=M:              Although occipere `to begin' appears to be a compound from coepere `to begin', it actually derives from
line=8603 tag=M:  ob `in the way of' + capere `to take', while coepere is from co- `together' + obsolete *apere `to fit'. The two verbs
line=8604 tag=M:  were routinely conflated (Simpson, p. 113), so it is no stretch to claim this example is directly analogous to (326).

doc_id=3642 igt_id=igt3642-187 13777 13792 L L L L B T T T T T B M T T T T+EX
language: Latin (lat)
line=13777 tag=L:  (69)     "Verborum vero figurae et mutatae sunt semper et, utcunque valuit conseutudo, mutantur.
line=13778 tag=L:           Itaque, si antiquum sermonem nostro comparemus, paene iam quidquid loquimur figura
line=13779 tag=L:           est, ut hac re invidere, not ut veteres et Cicero praecipue, hanc rem, et incumbere illi, non
line=13780 tag=L:           in illum, et plenum vino non vini, et huic on hunc adulari iam dicitur et mille alia."
line=13781 tag=B:
line=13782 tag=T:           `Figures of speech15 have always been subject to change and are constantly in the process
line=13783 tag=T:           of change in accordance with variations in usage. Consequently, when we compare the
line=13784 tag=T:           speech of our ancestors with our own, almost everything we say nowadays is a special
line=13785 tag=T:           mode of expression, i.e, variation in form. For example, we say hac re [ablative] invidere
line=13786 tag=T:           [to grudge a thing], whereas the ancients, and especially Cicero, said hanc rem
line=13787 tag=B:
line=13788 tag=M:                                             Chapter Four                                          283
line=13789 tag=T:           [accusative], and incumbere illi [dative; to lean upon him], not in illum [prepositional
line=13790 tag=T:           phrase with accusative], and plenum vino [ablative; full of wine] not vini [genitive], and
line=13791 tag=T:           huic [dative] not hunc [accusative] adulari [to flatter]. I could mention a thousand
line=13792 tag=T+EX:           others.' (emphasis mine)
